### `row_0000`
> Firms like Snapchat and Uber are valued highly despite not making profits because their valuations are based largely on what venture capitalists are willing to pay for a stake, which is driven partly by business plans, partly by guesses about the future, and mostly by what competing venture capitalists are willing to pay. [alce::row_0000::p14]
> Venture capitalists do expect eventual profitability, though not necessarily immediate returns. Companies like Uber are compared to Amazon, with the expectation that "with time, Uber will start making a profit." [alce::row_0000::p18]
> However, venture capitalists are increasingly impatient about this timeline. Larger investors and venture capitalists are sending a message to unprofitable companies: "Outline a solid path to profits and start producing them soon, or expect your valuation to be slashed." [alce::row_0000::p4]
> This pressure has already resulted in significant pre-IPO valuation drops for companies like Snapchat and Dropbox. [alce::row_0000::p4]

> Additionally, the cookie dough in commercial ice cream and most slice-and-bake cookie varieties are made with pasteurized eggs. [alce::row_0001::p12]

### `row_0002`
> Trash ends up in our oceans through several pathways. Single-use disposable items fall out of trash cans or are dropped on the ground by litterbugs, and when it rains, these items are washed into local streams that feed into the water. [alce::row_0002::p40]
> Regardless of where the trash originates, it all eventually ends up in the ocean. [alce::row_0002::p40]
> Ocean currents then push trash and plastic to gyres, which are areas where plastic accumulates. [alce::row_0002::p3]
> The scale of this problem is significant: approximately 10 percent of the roughly 200 billion pounds of plastic humanity produces every year ends up in our oceans. [alce::row_0002::p30]
> This amounts to about 8 million tons of plastic entering our oceans annually. [alce::row_0002::p3]

Cited: alce::row_0002::p3, alce::row_0002::p30, alce::row_0002::p40

### `row_0004`
> # Quantifiers, Constants, Variables, Terms, and Sentences in Logic

**Quantifiers** are logical operators used in first-order logic to express statements about objects; the symbol ∀ (for "all") is an example of a quantifier. [alce::row_0004::p56]
> **Constants** are symbols (usually the letters a, b, c) that are used to denote individual objects.
> **Variables** are symbols (usually the letters x, y, z) that can be replaced by constants or by complex formulas, and they are used in quantifying over individual objects.
> **Terms** are used to denote objects in the world being described and include both variables and constants. [alce::row_0004::p61]
> **Sentences** are used to express facts about the world. [alce::row_0004::p61]
> The key difference is that terms function as names or designators for objects (composed of constants and variables), while sentences are complete expressions that make claims about those objects. [alce::row_0004::p61]

Cited: alce::row_0004::p56, alce::row_0004::p61

### `row_0005`
> Pearls are rare in oysters because the formation of natural pearls requires a foreign object to randomly enter the oyster shell, which is an uncommon occurrence. [alce::row_0005::p34]
> It is estimated that only 1 in every 10,000 oysters produce a pearl. [alce::row_0005::p34]
> Additionally, natural pearls form when an intruder such as a bit of coral randomly enters the oyster, making this a chance event rather than a guaranteed process. [alce::row_0005::p9]

Cited: alce::row_0005::p34, alce::row_0005::p9

### `row_0006`

> However, this increase is temporary and occurs in different parts of the brain. [alce::row_0006::p15]
> When alcohol leaves the bloodstream, dopamine levels stop being elevated. [alce::row_0006::p4]
> Additionally, chronic alcohol use causes a decrease in dopamine transmission over time, which leads to withdrawal symptoms including cravings as the brain attempts to restore dopamine levels. [alce::row_0006::p12]

### `row_0007`
> When data is compressed or zipped, the data is re-encoded into a different, more compact representation that conveys the same information. [alce::row_0007::p18]
> Compression algorithms work by finding redundant parts of the data and removing those redundancies, or by identifying common pieces of data blocks that can be shrunk, removed, or substituted with smaller patterns. [alce::row_0007::p77]
> Compression does not physically cram the data into a smaller size package in any meaningful sense; instead, fewer words are used to convey the same meaning without actually saying the same thing. [alce::row_0007::p18]
> In lossless compression, such as ZIP files, no data is lost during the compression and decompression process. [alce::row_0007::p11]

Cited: alce::row_0007::p11, alce::row_0007::p18, alce::row_0007::p77

### `row_0008`
> Here are the main ways to compare living costs between cities and countries:

Use online comparison tools that show whether items like food, clothes, transportation, and entertainment are cheaper or more expensive than your current city, and by approximately how much. [alce::row_0008::p3]

> Do your own research before making conclusions, as individual cities may be more or less expensive than expected. [alce::row_0008::p65]

### `row_0011`
> # How a Nuclear Reactor/Power Plant Works

A nuclear power plant operates similarly to a thermoelectric power plant that uses fossil fuels, except it generates heat through nuclear fission rather than burning coal, oil, or gas. [alce::row_0011::p17]
> Most nuclear power plants use Uranium-235, an unstable isotope of uranium. [alce::row_0011::p75]
> A neutron is used to split the Uranium-235 into smaller isotopes, which releases large amounts of heat energy. [alce::row_0011::p75]
> This heat energy is used to heat water and produce steam. [alce::row_0011::p75]
> In pressurized water reactors (PWRs), the most common reactor type, nuclear fission of the fuel elements heats water in the reactor to approximately 315°C (600°F), and the water is kept under constant pressure. [alce::row_0011::p7]
> The steam generated from the heated water is then used to drive turbines and generate electricity. [alce::row_0011::p75]
> To control or shut down a nuclear reactor, control rods are used. [alce::row_0011::p7]
> Approximately 90% of nuclear fission reactors worldwide are light water reactors, which generate electricity very efficiently. [alce::row_0011::p15]

Cited: alce::row_0011::p15, alce::row_0011::p17, alce::row_0011::p7, alce::row_0011::p75

### `row_0012`
> Products end in .99 rather than being rounded up due to the left-digit effect. [alce::row_0012::p29]
> Customers tend to round down to the next lowest monetary unit when they see prices, focusing on the leftmost digit first and forming an opinion on price before rational consideration catches up. [alce::row_0012::p29]
> By using a lower first digit (as in .99 pricing), retailers can make prices appear lower to consumers than if they rounded up to the next whole number. [alce::row_0012::p29]

Cited: alce::row_0012::p29

### `row_0016`
> Siri underperforms compared to Google Assistant and Alexa for several reasons:

First, Siri has not kept pace with continuous improvement cycles that its competitors have maintained. [alce::row_0016::p7]
> Newer voice assistants offer much more functionality than Siri, which simply answers questions based on associated Google searches, conducts conversation based on pre-recorded responses, and performs simple tasks such as setting reminders. [alce::row_0016::p36]
> Second, Siri has technical limitations compared to its competitors. Siri has lesser audible clarity compared to Google and Amazon's voice assistants. [alce::row_0016::p42]
> Additionally, both Alexa and Google Assistant can understand natural language, while Siri is far behind in this capability. [alce::row_0016::p7]
> Third, Siri provides less precise and specific results. When asked about specific flights, Siri shows websites in the results which are not specific and precise, whereas Alexa will show the exact flight with timings. [alce::row_0016::p42]
> Despite being the first intelligent voice assistant from a major smartphone maker, Siri has lost its first-mover advantage to newer voice assistants. [alce::row_0016::p36]

Cited: alce::row_0016::p36, alce::row_0016::p42, alce::row_0016::p7

### `row_0018`
> # Napoleon's Rise and Fall and His Impact on France and the World

## Rise to Power

Napoleon was born on August 15, 1769, and was commissioned in the French military in 1785, where he quickly advanced. [alce::row_0018::p90]
> His expertise in mobile artillery and the military tactic of "envelopment" made him one of the greatest military commanders of all time. [alce::row_0018::p90]
> The French Revolutionary and Napoleonic Wars pitted France against various coalitions of other European nations nearly continuously from 1792 onward, and the success of French forces made Napoleon a hero. [alce::row_0018::p17]
> In 1799, Napoleon staged a successful coup d'état and became First Consul. [alce::row_0018::p17]
> ## Impact on France and Europe

Napoleon was a product of the French Revolution who entrenched and exported certain revolutionary innovations while gutting others. [alce::row_0018::p4]
> Rather than leading to France's defeat, the wars allowed the revolutionary regime to expand beyond its borders and create client republics. [alce::row_0018::p17]
> Napoleon centralized the nation, minimized regional differences, and shifted all decisions to Paris. [alce::row_0018::p12]
> ## Fall and Legacy

Napoleon suddenly returned, seized control of France, raised an army, and marched on his enemies in the Hundred Days, which ended with his final defeat at the Battle of Waterloo in 1815, and his exile to a remote island. [alce::row_0018::p12]
> Although the monarchy was subsequently restored and the conservative Congress of Vienna reversed the political changes that occurred during the wars, many of the Revolutionary and Napoleonic reforms were kept in place. [alce::row_0018::p12]

Cited: alce::row_0018::p12, alce::row_0018::p17, alce::row_0018::p4, alce::row_0018::p90

### `row_0019`

> Watermelon was a major food among slaves and poor people throughout history because it is a hardy plant that is easy and cheap to care for and maintain. [alce::row_0019::p29]
> The association between watermelon and Black people is rooted in racist propaganda and imagery that dates back to the 1800s, in which Black people were depicted in racist stereotypes as having an insatiable appetite for watermelon. [alce::row_0019::p31]
> This racist trope has persisted into modern times. [alce::row_0019::p31]

Cited: alce::row_0019::p29, alce::row_0019::p31

### `row_0021`
> Based on the provided sources, here are the reasons hateful subreddits are allowed to exist on Reddit:

Reddit's leadership has defended controversial subreddits by citing free speech principles. [alce::row_0021::p42]
> Reddit is an advertising-funded website, and hateful subreddits are funded through the site's overall operations, even indirectly by visitors to other subreddits. [alce::row_0021::p19]
> As of 2015, Reddit's strategy has been to allow hateful subreddits to exist but confine them to isolated spaces with limited resources and no permission to promote aggressively across the site, which the sources suggest is intended to "choke out racists and bigots without giving them motivation to act as martyrs for free speech." [alce::row_0021::p67]
> Reddit's structural design gives subreddit creators ("squatters") ownership and moderation control, and Reddit admins only intervene in extreme circumstances or when a subreddit owner is inactive. [alce::row_0021::p41]

### `row_0022`
> ## How Muscular Knots Form

Muscle knots are the body's way of protecting damaged tissue. [alce::row_0022::p8]
> When muscles sustain injury, spasms occur, giving rise to a tight feel in the affected area. [alce::row_0022::p85]
> The injury creates micro-tearing of muscle tissue, which produces scar tissue. [alce::row_0022::p78]
> If left untreated, the muscle tissue will continue to lose elasticity and cause postural stress that is hard to reverse. [alce::row_0022::p78]
> ## How to Prevent Muscular Knots

Several prevention strategies can help avoid getting muscle knots in the first place. [alce::row_0022::p78]
> Diet and hydration are important—you should drink plenty of water and eat a healthy diet, while avoiding alcohol, sugary caffeinated drinks, processed and fast foods, as these all contribute to dehydration. [alce::row_0022::p78]
> Additionally, avoiding stress, overworking muscles, and maintaining proper sleeping positions can help prevent knots from forming. [alce::row_0022::p11]

Cited: alce::row_0022::p11, alce::row_0022::p78, alce::row_0022::p8, alce::row_0022::p85

### `row_0023`

> Many states also have laws against false advertising, which vary by state. [alce::row_0023::p59]

> State consumer protection laws typically allow consumers to sue businesses that engage in deceptive advertising. [alce::row_0023::p10]

### `row_0026`
> Based on the provided sources, I can address why many proposed government policies are unpopular:

According to one perspective, unpopular policies are implemented because the donor class and special interests have disproportionate influence over politicians, while the concerns of everyday people are regularly ignored. [alce::row_0026::p86]
> This represents a failure of representative democracy, as the gap between the will of the people and the interests of those in charge continues to widen. [alce::row_0026::p4]
> Additionally, the structure of policy-making itself contributes to unpopular policies. When benefits from a policy are concentrated among a few groups while costs are dispersed across many individuals, citizens lack sufficient incentive to understand or oppose the policy, allowing politicians to implement measures that are not in the public's interest. [alce::row_0026::p40]
> However, the sources also present an alternative view: some government officials may believe that unpopular policies are actually in the common good, even when they meet with fierce public resistance. [alce::row_0026::p10]

> # How Helicopters Work

> A helicopter's propeller blades generate lift by pushing air downward. [alce::row_0034::p40]
> The spinning of rotor blades creates torque that can make the helicopter spin in the opposite direction, so a small second rotating blade in the tail works against this torque to hold the helicopter straight. [alce::row_0034::p35]

Yes, it makes sense that the propellers have enough lift to lift a helicopter. A propeller, like an airplane wing, is an airfoil—a curved surface that can generate lift when air moves over it. [alce::row_0034::p1]

### `row_0049`
> # Literally vs. Figuratively

Literally means exactly, in a strict sense, or to the letter. [alce::row_0049::p6]
> For example, when someone says "I am literally foaming at the mouth," this means real foam is actually coming out of their mouth. [alce::row_0049::p6]
> Figuratively means in a metaphorical sense—not in a real sense but expressed through figures of speech. [alce::row_0049::p6]
> When someone says "I am figuratively foaming at the mouth," they are using the idiom to mean they are very angry, and no actual foam is present. [alce::row_0049::p6]
> The word literally is often used loosely in casual conversation to describe circumstances that cannot actually be meant literally. [alce::row_0049::p3]
> To keep writing precise and accurate, it is important to distinguish between these two terms and use them correctly. [alce::row_0049::p3]

Cited: alce::row_0049::p3, alce::row_0049::p6

### `row_0076`
> # VPNs, Proxies, and Anonymity over the Internet

**What are VPNs and how do they work?**

VPNs (Virtual Private Networks) enable you to surf securely, anonymously and confidentially on the web. [alce::row_0076::p36]
> VPNs work by linking two computers securely and privately over the internet, with requests sent to the VPN server which then processes them. [alce::row_0076::p36]
> VPN protection generally requires that you download a software product that works with the company's VPN services, which bounce your connections around the globe through various distributed networks, creating 'virtual' tunnels that burrow through the Internet landscape and create a random path to thwart traffic analysis. [alce::row_0076::p43]
> **What are proxy servers and how do they work?**

An anonymous proxy server functions as a liaison between your home network or computer and the Internet, requesting information on your behalf using its own IP address instead of yours, so only the proxy's IP address is revealed instead of your home IP address. [alce::row_0076::p43]
> **What are the purposes of using VPNs and proxies?**

The purpose of using both proxy servers and VPNs is to hide the identity of the user, or to bypass a specific geo-restriction. [alce::row_0076::p13]
> When used for Internet connectivity, VPN service also acts as a form of proxy and protects the user's real IP address from public display, making them an increasingly popular form of anonymity protection for ordinary internet users. [alce::row_0076::p78]

Cited: alce::row_0076::p13, alce::row_0076::p36, alce::row_0076::p43, alce::row_0076::p78

### `row_0090`
> The front brake is more effective than the rear brake because of weight transfer during braking. [alce::row_0090::p6]
> When the front brake is used, the rider's weight shifts forward due to inertia, which increases the pressure of the front wheel on the road surface and strengthens the friction between the front wheel and road. [alce::row_0090::p23]
> Simultaneously, this weight transfer reduces the pressure of the rear wheel on the road surface, limiting the traction available to the rear brake. [alce::row_0090::p23]
> As a result, using only the rear brake allows you to use only about 30% of the total braking force possible. [alce::row_0090::p55]

Cited: alce::row_0090::p23, alce::row_0090::p55, alce::row_0090::p6

> Movement from a moving wheel creates electricity through electromagnetic induction. When a wheel spins, the rotating elements are surrounded by a large magnet and coils of copper wire, and as the magnet rotates, it moves an electrical conductor through a magnetic field. [alce::row_0092::p29]

> In practical applications like hydropower systems, the moving water rotates a wheel or turbine, and this motion can be used to power an alternator or generator to create electricity. [alce::row_0092::p52]

### `row_0105`
> **What is Virtual Memory?**

Virtual memory is an optimization technique implemented by the operating system that gives an application program the impression that it has more memory than actually exists. [alce::row_0105::p1]
> It is a type of memory management system that harnesses more memory than is physically available by using hardware and software in lieu of physical memory. [alce::row_0105::p11]
> Virtual memory works by temporarily transferring data from random access memory (RAM) to disk storage. [alce::row_0105::p11]
> **Why is it Used?**

Virtual memory is used for several key reasons. First, it allows for the extended use of physical memory by using disk storage when RAM is full. [alce::row_0105::p11]
> Second, it enables multitasking by allowing multiple programs to run simultaneously; when physical memory is full, excess data from open programs can be stored on the hard drive. [alce::row_0105::p7]
> Third, virtual memory allows for memory protection, as each virtual address is translated to a physical address. [alce::row_0105::p11]
> Additionally, it helps avoid wastage by allowing the operating system to manage real memory resources so that all running programs get what they need without excess. [alce::row_0105::p43]

Cited: alce::row_0105::p1, alce::row_0105::p11, alce::row_0105::p43, alce::row_0105::p7

### `row_0122`
> The body directs blood flow through several mechanisms:

The heart is the primary driver of blood flow, generating cardiac output by rhythmically contracting and relaxing, which creates changes in regional pressures that ensure blood moves in one direction through the circulatory system. [alce::row_0122::p39]
> The inner ear balance system helps control blood flow and signals the cardiovascular system to direct blood flow in response to changes in body position. [alce::row_0122::p28]
> The body also directs blood flow based on metabolic demand—more blood is delivered to regions that are doing more work, such as the gastrointestinal system after eating or muscles during emergencies. [alce::row_0122::p45]
> Additionally, in response to cold, the body directs blood flow toward the most central and vital organs as a self-preservation mechanism. [alce::row_0122::p14]

### `row_0125`
> # Understanding Lupus

Lupus is a chronic inflammatory disease in which a person's own immune system attacks their own tissues and organs. [alce::row_0125::p22]
> It is a complex autoimmune disorder that leads to inflammation and damage to various body tissues. [alce::row_0125::p58]
> The specific effects on your friend will depend on which parts of her body are affected, as lupus can impact many different areas. [alce::row_0125::p58]
> Lupus can affect the joints, kidneys, skin, heart, lungs, blood vessels, and brain, though the organs affected are different for every person. [alce::row_0125::p58]
> The most common and serious form is systemic lupus erythematosus (SLE), which can affect multiple tissues and organs throughout the body. [alce::row_0125::p22]
> The condition causes symptoms that range from mild to life-threatening. [alce::row_0125::p75]

### `row_0162`
> # Ordinal vs Cardinal Measurements

**Cardinal measurements use quantitative scales, while ordinal measurements use qualitative rankings.**
> Cardinal utility is a quantitative measure that assigns numerical values to satisfaction, whereas ordinal utility is a qualitative measure based on ranking preferences. [alce::row_0162::p88]
> **A key distinguishing property of cardinal measurements is that the ratio of differences between measurements remains invariant regardless of how utilities are assigned.** [alce::row_0162::p24]
> In contrast, ordinal measures rank items from high to low or good to bad based on subjective evaluations, but do not establish how much more one item exceeds another. [alce::row_0162::p12]
> **Practical examples illustrate the difference: age measured in years is cardinal because a twenty-year-old has lived twice as long as a ten-year-old, whereas ordinal measurements cannot make such quantitative comparisons.** [alce::row_0162::p16]

Cited: alce::row_0162::p12, alce::row_0162::p16, alce::row_0162::p24, alce::row_0162::p88

### `row_0165`
> ## Definition of "Grim Trigger" in Game Theory

A grim trigger is a strategy that prescribes cooperation as the "normal choice" until a rival deviates; once that deviation occurs, the "punishment choice" (defection) persists for the rest of the game. [alce::row_0165::p0]
> More specifically, it is a policy where you start by cooperating and continue to cooperate as long as everyone has cooperated in the past, but if someone breaks the community agreement, then you defect forever. [alce::row_0165::p36]
> The threat of eternal conflict keeps everyone cooperating today as long as they care enough about the future. [alce::row_0165::p36]
> ## Example in International Relations

In international relations, the grim trigger strategy was exemplified by the Eisenhower administration's nuclear doctrine in early 1954, when Secretary of State J.F. Dulles outlined that if the Soviets or their allies acted aggressively in any part of the world, the result may be a full-scale nuclear attack against the Soviet Union. [alce::row_0165::p5]
> This represented exactly a "grim trigger" strategy: even the slightest deviation from mutual cooperation (not behaving aggressively) would be met with the maximum possible punishment. [alce::row_0165::p5]
> Another example occurred in bilateral relations between two countries where a single trigger event—a presidential visit—led to permanent defection, with each side seeming keener on retribution than bargaining. [alce::row_0165::p0]

Cited: alce::row_0165::p0, alce::row_0165::p36, alce::row_0165::p5

### `row_0166`
> CO2 is safe to drink in beverages because the FDA considers it "generally recognized as safe" and it is a non-toxic substance. [alce::row_0166::p12]
> Our bodies naturally produce CO2 while breathing, making it a natural substance. [alce::row_0166::p0]
> However, CO2 is not inherently toxic to breathe in normal amounts—the issue arises with concentration and displacement of oxygen. When CO2 gets loose in liquid form, it creates an invisible puddle on the ground that excludes oxygen, rapidly killing everything in its path. [alce::row_0166::p80]
> The danger comes from relative changes in oxygen levels rather than CO2 toxicity itself; relative changes in O2 lead to more toxic effects than relative changes in CO2. [alce::row_0166::p23]
> The distinction is therefore one of dose and context: small amounts of CO2 in beverages are safe, but high concentrations of CO2 that displace oxygen in the air can be lethal by asphyxiation.

Cited: alce::row_0166::p0, alce::row_0166::p12, alce::row_0166::p23, alce::row_0166::p80

### `row_0172`
> Clicker training is a training method that emphasizes positive reinforcement and operant conditioning. [alce::row_0172::p3]
> Here's how it works: A clicker (or any other tool that makes a distinctive sound) is used along with a reward, such as a food-based treat, to teach your dog to repeatedly perform a desired behavior. [alce::row_0172::p20]
> The click serves as an "event marker" that marks the precise moment the dog does what you want. [alce::row_0172::p24]
> This signal pinpoints the behavior exactly, which allows your dog to quickly realize what it is that you want them to do and then they do it — quickly and enthusiastically. [alce::row_0172::p3]
> Clicker training works by using positive reinforcement, so there's no punishment involved, which means your dog will actually want to do what you say. [alce::row_0172::p20]
> The clicker must be used every time a command is given, and the same treat must always follow, regardless of who is training the dog.
> However, it's important to note that while many trainers believe in clickers, there is not a substantial scientific literature demonstrating that clicker training is definitively more effective than other methods. [alce::row_0172::p22]

> However, employees may also attempt to claim that their refusal to work with certain co-workers is "protected concerted activity," though this is a separate legal claim that would need to meet specific criteria. [alce::row_0177::p9]
> In general, a blanket refusal to work with a specific co-worker for reasons unrelated to safety would not be legally protected.
> The sources provided focus specifically on refusals based on safety concerns or protected concerted activity, not on personal preferences or other non-safety reasons.

### `row_0188`
> Weber meant rationalization as the pursuit of a specific goal in a self-conscious and systematically organized manner. [alce::row_0188::p1]
> More simply, rationality according to Weber is the practice of appropriating means to an end. [alce::row_0188::p1]
> Weber applied this concept to describe modern institutions: he characterized modern capitalism as the rationalized economy, bureaucracy as the rationalized organization, and the modern state as based on formal procedures and rules of rational-legal authority. [alce::row_0188::p35]
> Importantly, rationalization involves means-end calculation where the "rationality" consists in the fact that the "goal" is a "quantifiable" one, rather than in the goals themselves. [alce::row_0188::p18]
> However, Weber did not use "rationalization" as a term of praise and was quite ambivalent and critical about rationally organized action and a totally rationalized society as the ultimate goal of modernity. [alce::row_0188::p1]

> When a bullet is fired, gravity immediately begins pulling it downward the instant it leaves the barrel. [alce::row_0235::p58]

> Remarkably, these complex structures can be built in about 20 minutes with no planning or leader directing the effort. [alce::row_0238::p10]

### `row_0241`

### `row_0242`
> # Flicker Fusion Threshold

The flicker fusion threshold is the frequency at which a blinking light appears to be stable or when an intermittent light stimulus appears to be completely steady to the human eye. [alce::row_0242::p2]
> It is the speed at which a series of static images appear to become a steady moving image, or when a flashing light flashes on and off quickly enough that we see a steady beam. [alce::row_0242::p0]
> The average flicker fusion threshold for human beings is 16 Hertz for 50% of test subjects, meaning if a light flashes on and off 16 times per second, 50% of people will see it as steady. [alce::row_0242::p0]
> The human eye is capable of distinguishing between intermittent stimuli such as flickering light up to a threshold, which is usually around 16 Hertz. [alce::row_0242::p1]
> The flicker fusion threshold changes with several parameters. It varies with luminance, color, and psychological state such as fatigue. [alce::row_0242::p2]
> The threshold is higher when the light source is brighter. [alce::row_0242::p6]
> Additionally, it varies between individuals depending on their eyesight. [alce::row_0242::p1]
> The flicker fusion threshold is a relative rather than an absolute quantity, defined as the frequency at which flicker is detected in 50% of the tests. [alce::row_0242::p6]

Cited: alce::row_0242::p0, alce::row_0242::p1, alce::row_0242::p2, alce::row_0242::p6

### `row_0248`
> # Why Rich People Keep Personal Assets in Holding Companies

Rich people keep their personal assets in holding companies primarily for **asset protection**.
> When assets are owned by a holding company rather than personally, creditors cannot claim those assets even if they have a legal judgment against the individual. [alce::row_0248::p86]
> This is because a personal creditor cannot claim properties owned by an LLC since the individual does not own the property personally. [alce::row_0248::p40]
> The structure works by separating personal liability from asset ownership.
> Rich people do not own things directly; instead, their companies own things, which means that even if anyone sues them personally, there are no personal assets to take.
> Additionally, holding companies allow for the separation of liability-producing assets from non-liability producing assets such as cash and savings. [alce::row_0248::p86]
> The rich also use holding companies for **financial secrecy** and to protect themselves from potential lawsuits related to their business operations. [alce::row_0248::p68]

Cited: alce::row_0248::p40, alce::row_0248::p68, alce::row_0248::p86

### `row_0254`
> # The 555 Timer Circuit

A 555 timer is an integrated circuit (chip) that is extremely popular and versatile in electronics. [alce::row_0254::p3]
> Its main uses include functioning as a timer and as a multivibrator. [alce::row_0254::p3]
> The 555 timer takes its name from the fact that the original model had three resistors, each rated at five thousand ohms. [alce::row_0254::p3]
> The 555 timer was invented by Hans Camenzind and was first introduced in 1971 by Signetics. [alce::row_0254::p1]
> At the time of its 1971 release, the 555 timer was the only commercially available timer circuit. [alce::row_0254::p3]
> The original model was formally known as the SE555/NE555 and was marketed as the IC Time Machine. [alce::row_0254::p3]
> The 555 can be used to provide time delays, as an oscillator, and as a flip-flop element. [alce::row_0254::p1]
> It is frequently used in astable mode to generate a continuous series of pulses, but can also be used to make a one-shot or monostable circuit. [alce::row_0254::p15]
> The 555 can source or sink 200 mA of output current and is capable of driving a wide range of output devices. [alce::row_0254::p15]
> The 555 timer remains in widespread use today due to its low price, ease of use, and good stability. [alce::row_0254::p1]
> It has been in use since 1972 and is still used in thousands of designs across both professional and hobbyist equipment. [alce::row_0254::p10]

Cited: alce::row_0254::p1, alce::row_0254::p10, alce::row_0254::p15, alce::row_0254::p3

### `row_0263`
> # What an API Key Is and How to Use It

## What an API Key Is

An API key is a string of characters used to authenticate to the Platform API. [alce::row_0263::p82]
> In plain English, an API key is a unique value that identifies you. [alce::row_0263::p55]
> It functions as a form of secret token submitted alongside web service requests in order to identify the origin of the request. [alce::row_0263::p55]
> An API key refers specifically to your account and performs a similar function to your username and password. [alce::row_0263::p82]
> ## How to Use It

To use the Platform API, you will need an API key. [alce::row_0263::p82]
> When using API calls to access your workflows, you must pass the API keys for authentication. [alce::row_0263::p12]
> A request that does not provide an OAuth 2.0 token must send an API key. [alce::row_0263::p87]
> The system uses a combination of your API key and a JSON Web Token (JWT) to manage authentication, where your API key is used to retrieve a JWT that can then be used to authenticate API calls. [alce::row_0263::p12]
> ## Important Security Considerations

API keys can be time-limited and can be revoked immediately if they are ever compromised, making them far safer to use than revealing your username and password. [alce::row_0263::p82]
> You should not share the API key with anyone you wouldn't be comfortable sharing all your account data with, since through the API all data is accessible from your account. [alce::row_0263::p62]

Cited: alce::row_0263::p12, alce::row_0263::p55, alce::row_0263::p62, alce::row_0263::p82, alce::row_0263::p87

### `row_0268`
> # Differences Between Tone, Pitch, and Melody

**Pitch** is the actual note or frequency you are singing, representing the "highness" or "lowness" of a sound. [alce::row_0268::p0]
> Pitch is created according to the speed at which your vocal cords vibrate. [alce::row_0268::p0]
> **Tone** is the color or quality of a pitch. [alce::row_0268::p0]
> Tones can be described using words like warm, bright, strident, clear, ringing, rich, shrill, breathy, or smoky. [alce::row_0268::p0]
> Tone is partly innate and personal to each person, but different tones can also be developed as vocal effects. [alce::row_0268::p0]
> **Melody** is a series of tones or sounds arranged in sequence, responded to based on differences in pitch height and rise and fall. [alce::row_0268::p22]
> Melodies are relationships of successive pitches across time that the brain can learn to recognize, even when the melody is in a different key. [alce::row_0268::p2]
> A melody is often described as the horizontal portion of a song. [alce::row_0268::p4]
> In summary, pitch is the frequency of a note, tone is the quality or color of that pitch, and melody is a sequence of pitches arranged over time.

Cited: alce::row_0268::p0, alce::row_0268::p2, alce::row_0268::p22, alce::row_0268::p4

### `row_0271`
> **What is pneumonia?**

Pneumonia is an infection of the lungs that attacks air sacs in the lungs, causing them to become inflamed and sometimes filled with fluid. [alce::row_0271::p28]
> The air sacs are responsible for moving oxygen from the lungs to the blood, so pneumonia results in breathing problems and lowers the level of oxygen in the blood. [alce::row_0271::p28]
> **Why is it deadlier for elderly people?**

The elderly are more susceptible to pneumonia than younger people for several reasons. [alce::row_0271::p25]
> Aging causes a decrease in the immune system response, so an elderly person's defenses are weaker. [alce::row_0271::p58]
> Additionally, seniors may already be suffering from comorbid conditions such as heart disease, meaning they don't tolerate infection as well as younger people. [alce::row_0271::p58]
> The complications resulting from pneumonia in the elderly can be life-threatening, ranging from low blood pressure and kidney failure to bacteremia, an infection that spreads to the bloodstream. [alce::row_0271::p58]
> The elderly are also more likely to be hospitalized for pneumonia and need mechanical ventilation, resulting in a longer hospital stay than younger persons. [alce::row_0271::p25]

Cited: alce::row_0271::p25, alce::row_0271::p28, alce::row_0271::p58

### `row_0299`
> Chirality is the property of an object or molecule being different from its mirror image. [alce::row_0299::p50]
> The term "handedness" describes this fundamental characteristic—the existence of left/right opposition. [alce::row_0299::p50]
> To understand what's actually happening: your left and right hands appear similar, but they are only mirror images of each other and cannot be superimposed on top of each other. [alce::row_0299::p2]
> This same principle applies to molecules—they can be chiral if they contain certain structural features that make them non-superimposable with their mirror image. [alce::row_0299::p2]
> The right-hand/left-hand metaphor works because your hands are the most intuitive example of chirality in everyday life. [alce::row_0299::p50]
> Just as you cannot perfectly overlay your left hand onto your right hand no matter how you rotate it, chiral molecules cannot be perfectly aligned with their mirror-image versions. [alce::row_0299::p2]
> In biological systems, this matters significantly: proteins and DNA possess unique 3-dimensional shapes that depend on chirality, and these shapes are essential for biochemical processes to work correctly in our bodies. [alce::row_0299::p29]

> # Weight Loss and the Role of Genetics in a Calorie-In/Calorie-Out Diet

While calories in versus calories out is a basic rule of thermodynamics for weight loss, the relationship is more complicated than this simple model suggests. [alce::row_0330::p20]
> Genetics play a role in weight loss outcomes. [alce::row_0330::p20]
> Some people have genetic variations that make it more difficult to lose weight on a calorie-restricted diet compared to others. [alce::row_0330::p0]

> Additionally, different macronutrients have different effects on metabolic rate—carbs and fats do not have equal metabolic impacts despite containing calories. [alce::row_0330::p6]

### `row_0340`
> # What x Approaching Infinity and Zero Means in Calculus

When x approaches zero, the behavior of a function depends on its form. For example, with the function 1/x, as x approaches zero, 1/x approaches infinity. [alce::row_0340::p6]
> When x approaches positive infinity, you should focus on the leading term (highest power term) in a polynomial to determine the limit, as the leading term grows most quickly and lower terms will not affect the limit value. [alce::row_0340::p67]
> The leading term will grow toward either positive infinity or negative infinity depending on its form. [alce::row_0340::p67]
> When x approaches negative infinity, similarly, you focus on the leading term of a polynomial to determine whether it grows toward positive infinity or negative infinity as x gets extremely large in the negative direction. [alce::row_0340::p67]
> In general, when dividing by zero (which occurs as x approaches zero in certain functions), using calculus you see it's a number that approaches infinity, either positive infinity or negative infinity. [alce::row_0340::p0]

Cited: alce::row_0340::p0, alce::row_0340::p6, alce::row_0340::p67

### `row_0341`
> Many people say there is no such thing as centrifugal force because it is an imaginary force that does not actually exist. [alce::row_0341::p13]
> According to Newton's Third Law, for a force to occur, an equal and opposite force must exist as well, and there is no equal and opposite force for "centrifugal force."
> In reality, what people perceive as centrifugal force is actually the result of objects wanting to continue moving in a straight line rather than being pushed outward by an actual force. [alce::row_0341::p13]
> The actual force at work is centripetal force, which is an inward force that keeps objects from traveling in a straight line and confines them to circular motion around an axis. [alce::row_0341::p13]

### `row_0351`
> # How SVG and Other Vector Graphics Work

SVG stands for Scalable Vector Graphics, an XML markup language for creating two-dimensional images using vectors. [alce::row_0351::p68]
> Vector graphics use polygons—plane figures bounded by a finite chain of straight line segments closing in a loop—to represent images, which is different from raster graphics that use a rectangular grid of pixels. [alce::row_0351::p4]
> As a vector graphics format, SVG uses precisely-defined geometric shapes to build an image. [alce::row_0351::p88]
> A key advantage of vector graphics is that vector images can be infinitely enlarged without any noticeable decrease in quality, whereas raster images do not work this way. [alce::row_0351::p4]
> When used on web pages, SVG images provide an infinite level of detail, so they look sharp regardless of screen size or pixel density. [alce::row_0351::p68]
> SVG graphics can be shaded, textured, built from partially-transparent overlapping layers, or even filled with photographic images. [alce::row_0351::p88]
> Most modern web browsers support SVG.

Cited: alce::row_0351::p4, alce::row_0351::p68, alce::row_0351::p88

### `row_0371`
> Lightning strikes are detected using ground-based sensing stations and networks that capture and process the data for online display.

More than 100 remote, ground-based sensing stations located across the U.S. detect cloud-to-ground and intra-cloud lightning flashes and map them in real-time through the National Lightning Detection Network. [alce::row_0371::p32]
> These remote sensors send raw data via a satellite-based communications network to a Network Control Center, which processes information on the location, time, polarity, and amplitude of each strike within seconds. [alce::row_0371::p32]
> In other systems, when at least five stations record a pulse from a lightning strike, computers triangulate the arrival times at different stations to pinpoint the location. [alce::row_0371::p0]
> The lightning information is then communicated to users and displayed on online maps, such as those showing strikes from the most recent 30 minutes in Google Earth or overlaid on NASA cloud maps. [alce::row_0371::p0]

### `row_0375`
> Killing someone is not automatically murder in all contexts, and war crimes are distinguished from ordinary crimes by specific legal and contextual factors.

Killing in wartime is not necessarily murder because the context and legality differ from civilian homicide. [alce::row_0375::p17]
> Killing someone who is actively trying to kill you in a battlefield context "isn't 'murder' and it isn't illegal." [alce::row_0375::p17]

For example, intentionally killing a protected person becomes the "war crime" of terrorism (rather than simple murder) when that harm is "calculated to influence or affect the conduct of government or civilian population by intimidation or coercion." [alce::row_0375::p41]
> Wars can be fought for legitimate or illegitimate reasons, and this affects whether killings constitute murder or lawful acts. [alce::row_0375::p43]
> The nature of the crime also matters—for instance, genocide (systematically killing people based on ethnicity) is fundamentally different in nature from killing combatants in siege and bombardment. [alce::row_0375::p12]

### `row_0382`
> A paper towel acts like a siphon between two unequal cups through the combined action of capillary action and gravity. The paper towel has tiny pores within it which act as capillaries, and the adhesive forces between water molecules and paper towel molecules cause water to rise through the paper towel. [alce::row_0382::p0]
> Capillary action uses the meniscus force to lift the water to the highest point of the paper towel. [alce::row_0382::p10]
> Once the water reaches the top of the paper towel, it can flow downhill into the empty cup, causing the paper towel to function as a siphon. [alce::row_0382::p10]

Cited: alce::row_0382::p0, alce::row_0382::p10

### `row_0383`
> **What causes lightning:**

Lightning is caused by electrical charge buildup in storms. Storms create a negative charge that is attracted to positive charges on the ground, which channels lightning. [alce::row_0383::p40]
> **Why rainstorms often cause lightning while snowstorms rarely do:**

Nearly three-quarters (70%) of lightning strikes occur during a rainstorm. [alce::row_0383::p23]
> The conditions that cause rain also may cause clouds to store electrical energy, and that stored energy becomes available to create lightning. [alce::row_0383::p23]
> Thunderstorms with lightning do happen more often when air temperatures are warm. [alce::row_0383::p16]
> Hot air rises, and as it cools and condenses into water droplets, if the air is warm enough and there's enough atmospheric instability, favorable conditions for lightning and thunder occur. [alce::row_0383::p25]
> In contrast, lightning during snowstorms is possible but requires more extreme conditions—specifically when a really intense cold front slams into warmer air. [alce::row_0383::p16]
> Thundersnow requires either a normal thunderstorm on the leading edge of a cold or warm front in a winter environment, a heavy synoptic snowstorm with strong vertical mixing, or lake effect/ocean effect thunderstorms. [alce::row_0383::p56]

Cited: alce::row_0383::p16, alce::row_0383::p23, alce::row_0383::p25, alce::row_0383::p40, alce::row_0383::p56

### `row_0396`

### `row_0398`
> I'd be happy to help clarify membrane potential for you!

**What is membrane potential?**

Membrane potential is the electrical potential difference (voltage) across a cell's plasma membrane. [alce::row_0398::p1]
> In simpler terms, it's a difference in electric charge between the inside and outside of the cell. [alce::row_0398::p1]
> **Why does this charge separation exist?**

A membrane potential arises when there is a difference in the electrical charge on the two sides of a membrane, due to a slight excess of positive ions on one side and a slight excess of negative ions on the other. [alce::row_0398::p12]
> This charge separation is maintained by ion transporters embedded in the membrane, which keep different ion concentrations inside versus outside the cell. [alce::row_0398::p2]
> **What does "polarized" mean?**

When the positive and negative charges are equally balanced on each side of the membrane, the membrane is electrically neutral and no membrane potential exists. [alce::row_0398::p12]
> Conversely, when charges are unequal (separated), the membrane is "polarized"—meaning it has an electrical imbalance.

**The key insight:**

A potential difference simply means that electrical charges are being held apart by the membrane, creating a voltage across it. [alce::row_0398::p1]
> This is why it's called "membrane potential"—the membrane is the barrier that keeps the charges separated and maintains this electrical difference.

Cited: alce::row_0398::p1, alce::row_0398::p12, alce::row_0398::p2

### `row_0399`

While campaign contributions are generally legal in the United States when made within limits and according to guidelines, they can constitute bribery where a corrupt bargain or quid pro quo arrangement exists between the donor and the recipient. [alce::row_0399::p34]
> The key distinction is that campaign contributions given merely as support or appreciation for official actions are not bribes, but those given in exchange for specific official actions in return cross the line into bribery. [alce::row_0399::p34]
> Courts have recognized that politicians are aware of their contributors' identities and business interests, which can inspire donations with corrupt intent. [alce::row_0399::p34]

### `row_0407`
> News reports use the word "allegedly" even when there is proof of something for several reasons:

First, news outlets use "allegedly" when there is only one source reporting the information and no secondary independent source to verify it. [alce::row_0407::p0]
> This practice reflects reporters being careful to be accurate rather than making unfounded claims.

Second, the word "allegedly" serves a legal protection function. When reporting accusations of crime, news media use "allegedly" to report someone else's accusation without stating their own opinion, which helps protect against defamation liability. [alce::row_0407::p79]
> Third, "allegedly" is used as a cautious approach when there are conflicting claims from different parties. [alce::row_0407::p0]
> Both statements from different sources have to be treated as truth claims until properly verified, which is why similar cautious language like "reportedly," "claimed," and "rumored" are employed.

Cited: alce::row_0407::p0, alce::row_0407::p79

### `row_0426`
> # Differences Between Editorial, Sponsored Article, News Article, and Related Content

**Editorial vs. News Article:**
News articles are products of reporters' observation and research that intend to inform readers with factual information. [alce::row_0426::p3]
> Editorials, by contrast, represent opinions rather than objective reporting. [alce::row_0426::p3]
> Typically, editorials appear in a specially labeled section to make it easier for readers to distinguish news articles from opinion pieces. [alce::row_0426::p3]
> **Sponsored Content vs. Editorial:**
The only difference between a regular editorial article and sponsored content is the denotation of it being sponsored.
> Sponsored content must be given visual cues and labeled with wording that clearly demonstrates a commercial arrangement. [alce::row_0426::p22]
> **The Challenge of Distinguishing Them:**
The lines between paid-for/sponsored and editorial content are becoming increasingly blurred. [alce::row_0426::p10]
> Consumers often find it difficult to distinguish between editorial and sponsored content; in one study, when 500 consumers were shown sponsored content, a strong majority in four out of six groups thought the ad was an article. [alce::row_0426::p22]
> **Native Advertising:**
Native advertising is a type of sponsored content that is naturally in-feed and looks organic to the editorial content.
> This allows advertisers to get their content in front of larger audiences.

Cited: alce::row_0426::p10, alce::row_0426::p22, alce::row_0426::p3

### `row_0438`
> # What Folding@Home Does

Folding@home is a distributed computing project that studies protein folding and misfolding. [alce::row_0438::p39]
> The project's goal is to add greater understanding to protein folding, misfolding, aggregation, and related diseases such as Alzheimer's, Parkinson's, Mad Cow disease, and some cancers. [alce::row_0438::p20]
> # How You Help

You help by running a small piece of software on your computer or game console. [alce::row_0438::p39]
> The Folding@home client runs in the background of your personal computer, taking advantage of unused CPU power while your computer is idle. [alce::row_0438::p88]
> The client periodically connects to a server to retrieve packets of data called "work units," performs calculations on protein structures, and sends the results back to the server when done. [alce::row_0438::p88]
> In essence, your participation is a donation of your free compute time to the research effort. [alce::row_0438::p47]
> By having thousands of personal computer users contribute their idle processing power, Folding@home can perform computationally intensive simulations that would otherwise take years, completing them in a few weeks or months instead. [alce::row_0438::p80]
> This allows researchers to study biomedical questions that were formerly considered impractical to tackle computationally. [alce::row_0438::p80]

Cited: alce::row_0438::p20, alce::row_0438::p39, alce::row_0438::p47, alce::row_0438::p80, alce::row_0438::p88

### `row_0439`
> People with short-term memory loss face a specific challenge: they can understand something happening and have no problem with older memories, but cannot turn those short-term memories into long-term memories. [alce::row_0439::p64]
> The normal process of forming long-term memories involves consolidation in the hippocampus, a brain structure in the medial temporal lobe; once consolidated, memories become available in the neocortex where they can be retrieved. [alce::row_0439::p69]
> For people with short-term memory loss, the key factors that can help form long-term memories are repetition and attention. The more attention one pays to something in short-term memory, the more likely it is to be stored in long-term memory. [alce::row_0439::p44]
> Additionally, repetition can help hold things in short-term memory and increase the probability of that information entering permanent storage in long-term memory. [alce::row_0439::p44]
> However, if someone has damage to the hippocampus, they have difficulty forming new memories because the hippocampus is essential for converting short-term memory into long-term memory. [alce::row_0439::p74]

### `row_0449`
> # How Vitamin D Works

Vitamin D is not actually a vitamin in the traditional sense, but rather a hormone that your body produces. [alce::row_0449::p0]
> When your skin is exposed to ultraviolet B (UVB) radiation from the sun, vitamin D is synthesized. [alce::row_0449::p87]
> Specifically, UVB rays stimulate the production of vitamin D3 by reacting with a cholesterol substance in the skin called 7-dehydrocholesterol. [alce::row_0449::p15]
> After synthesis in the skin, your body undergoes several chemical processes to activate the vitamin. [alce::row_0449::p17]
> The fat-soluble vitamin is transported to the liver and kidneys where it is converted into the forms your tissues need, becoming activated vitamin D. [alce::row_0449::p87]
> Once activated, vitamin D works by regulating calcium in the bones, blood, and gut, and helps cells to function properly. [alce::row_0449::p17]
> # Is It Real and Where Does It Come From?

Vitamin D is a real substance that your body creates from sun exposure. [alce::row_0449::p0]
> Your body can create the hormone form of vitamin D from the sun, though another form is a nutrient that can be found in certain foods such as fish, eggs, and mushrooms. [alce::row_0449::p12]
> The primary source of vitamin D is the sun, as vitamin D present in some foods is not enough to meet daily requirements. [alce::row_0449::p15]

### `row_0474`
> When you have a fever, you feel freezing cold and shiver because your body's internal thermostat in the hypothalamus has been raised to a higher setting. [alce::row_0474::p3]
> Since your actual body temperature is now below this new thermostat setting, your body perceives itself as cold. [alce::row_0474::p3]
> Blood vessels in your skin tighten and shrink, keeping warm blood deeper within your body and making your skin feel cold. [alce::row_0474::p6]
> As a result, your muscles contract and you shiver. [alce::row_0474::p6]
> The shivering generates more heat to bring your body temperature up to match the elevated thermostat setting. [alce::row_0474::p43]
> Additionally, the hypothalamus signals for norepinephrine to be released by the adrenal glands, which causes increased burning of brown adipose tissue and increases in muscle tone and shivering, all aimed at raising your body temperature to the new thermostat setting. [alce::row_0474::p43]

### `row_0476`
> The sources explain the cost difference between green and red laser pointers versus laser levels through different mechanisms.

For laser pointers, the cost difference is relatively modest because green lasers appear much brighter to the human eye due to human eye sensitivity. The human eye is approximately 80 times more sensitive to green light than red light, so green laser pointers appear significantly brighter at the same power output, justifying only a modest price premium of 10 to 20 times more expensive than red pointers. [alce::row_0476::p10]
> For laser levels, the cost difference is much larger because manufacturing green laser levels requires substantially more complex and expensive components. To manufacture a green laser beam, a green laser diode is required at extra cost, and you may need up to 6 diodes to make a multiline green laser, which significantly bloats the overall cost compared to making a red laser version. [alce::row_0476::p50]
> Additionally, green laser diodes are more susceptible to heat effects, making them more expensive to produce reliably. [alce::row_0476::p50]
> In summary, green laser pointers cost only moderately more because they leverage human eye sensitivity to appear brighter without requiring dramatically different technology, while green laser levels cost hundreds of dollars more because they require multiple expensive diodes and more complex thermal management compared to their red counterparts.

Cited: alce::row_0476::p10, alce::row_0476::p50

### `row_0478`
> ## Why It Is Important to Measure Your Heart Rate While Exercising

Monitoring your heart rate during exercise helps you determine your maximum heart rate, which can be used to identify the type of exercise or workout that is good for you from an aerobic point of view. [alce::row_0478::p7]
> It also serves as an indicator of how fit you are. [alce::row_0478::p7]
> When you keep track of your heart rate during exercise, you are able to ensure the safety of your fitness program by staying within normal ranges. [alce::row_0478::p19]
> Another good reason to record your heart rate during exercise is to gauge your progress and help you achieve your desired goals. [alce::row_0478::p19]
> The whole point of measuring your heart rate is to help you exercise at an intensity that is challenging to you, but not so hard that you exhaust yourself too quickly. [alce::row_0478::p5]
> ## Practical and Advanced Applications

**Practical Applications:**

Beginning exercisers might feel comfortable exercising at about 60% of their maximum heart rate, while more advanced exercisers can probably exercise at the 85% level or higher. [alce::row_0478::p5]
> Exercising at 75% of your maximum heart rate will make you moderately aerobically fit. [alce::row_0478::p5]
> A reduction of heart rate after performing the same exercises indicates an improvement in fitness and sporting performance. [alce::row_0478::p12]
> **Advanced Applications:**

It is important that an accurate heart rate can be measured while the patient is actually exercising, as the heart rate while exercising provides a marker of both whole body and myocardial strain. [alce::row_0478::p25]

Cited: alce::row_0478::p12, alce::row_0478::p19, alce::row_0478::p25, alce::row_0478::p5, alce::row_0478::p7

> Localized programming languages exist that translate programming language syntax into other languages, such as করো (*koro*), which localizes the Go programming language into Bengali.
> The goal is to allow developers to collaborate on open-source projects even when they don't speak the same human language.
> So you're not being ignorant—English is indeed the practical standard for most collaborative programming projects currently, but this is an area where the programming community is working toward more multilingual solutions.

### `row_0496`
> Three-parent children result from mitochondrial replacement therapy, a technique that involves genetic material from three sources. [alce::row_0496::p13]
> Mitochondria are organelles in cells that produce energy and contain their own DNA called mtDNA, which is inherited separately from nuclear DNA. [alce::row_0496::p13]
> In mitochondrial replacement, part of an egg from a donor woman is added to the egg of the mother to prevent mitochondrial disease from being passed to the child. [alce::row_0496::p37]
> Although mtDNA represents only a tiny part of a child's genetic makeup—37 genes compared to about 23,000 genes from the cell's other DNA—it is still part of the child's genetics. [alce::row_0496::p77]
> This means the child ultimately has three genetic parents: the mother and father who contribute nuclear DNA, and the donor who contributes the mitochondrial DNA. [alce::row_0496::p77]
> A helpful way to understand this is that two parents gave the child a car, but the third parent built the engine. [alce::row_0496::p77]

### `row_0508`
> Several theories explain why humans are mostly hairless compared to other land mammals:

One theory suggests that human hairlessness evolved as an adaptation to hot climates, since sweating to regulate body temperature works far more efficiently in hairless individuals than in furred animals that must slow down and hyperventilate to cool themselves. [alce::row_0508::p3]
> Another theory proposes that humans lost their fur as a defense against parasites like ticks and fleas, which would be difficult to spot and remove if hidden within dense hair. [alce::row_0508::p77]
> A third theory relates to human semi-aquatic ancestry, noting that most aquatic mammals are hairless because fur is largely dead weight in water, and this evolutionary history may explain human hairlessness. [alce::row_0508::p0]
> Additionally, Charles Darwin hypothesized that human hairlessness resulted from sexual selection, particularly when prehistoric males selected for hairless females, a trait that subsequently affected males through genetic correlation between the sexes. [alce::row_0508::p38]
> Finally, hairlessness may have provided an endurance hunting advantage, allowing mostly hairless human hunters to sweat effectively while pursuing prey that needed to rest and pant to avoid overheating. [alce::row_0508::p71]

### `row_0515`
> # How Rain, Clouds, and Related Weather Work

**How clouds form and stay in the atmosphere:**
Water droplets in the air condense and cool on tiny particles such as dust in the atmosphere, forming ice crystals inside clouds. [alce::row_0515::p80]
> Clouds form in the atmosphere when water condenses after energy from the sun causes water to evaporate from the oceans. [alce::row_0515::p66]
> **How water stays in clouds:**
Droplets remain suspended in clouds until they become too heavy. [alce::row_0515::p66]
> Some droplets collide with others to become larger, while others grow as water condenses out of the air into the droplet. [alce::row_0515::p65]
> **How rain forms and falls:**
When drops become too heavy to stay in the cloud, rain falls. [alce::row_0515::p65]
> Once water droplets grow large and heavy enough, they fall. [alce::row_0515::p80]
> The drop sizes get too big and the drops are too heavy to stay in the atmosphere, causing rain. [alce::row_0515::p66]
> **Size of raindrops:**
The size of raindrops is highly variable, ranging from as small as 0.5mm in diameter to 6mm. [alce::row_0515::p65]
> **The water cycle:**
Clouds move with prevailing winds until the drops are too heavy to stay in the atmosphere, causing rain, which then cycles again through the hydrologic cycle. [alce::row_0515::p66]

Cited: alce::row_0515::p65, alce::row_0515::p66, alce::row_0515::p80

### `row_0519`
> # What is Linux, What Does It Do, & Why Is It So Popular?

## What is Linux?

Linux is an operating system that is very much like UNIX and has become very popular over the last several years. [alce::row_0519::p1]
> It is the leading operating system on servers and other big systems such as mainframe computers. [alce::row_0519::p37]
> ## What Does It Do?

An operating system is the first piece of software that the computer executes when you turn the machine on. [alce::row_0519::p1]
> The operating system loads itself into memory and begins managing the resources available on the computer. [alce::row_0519::p1]
> Linux helps in designing software, servers, and supercomputers. [alce::row_0519::p85]
> ## Why Is It So Popular?

Linux is the most popular example of open-source software. [alce::row_0519::p10]
> Open source is publicly shared intellectual property, and every aspect of the operating system's source code is "open" and does not have licensing restrictions that limit use, modification, contributions, or redistribution. [alce::row_0519::p10]
> Additionally, Linux is the more secure, robust, highest performing operating system on the planet. [alce::row_0519::p10]
> Linux systems are used throughout computing, including in internet of things and smart devices. [alce::row_0519::p37]

Cited: alce::row_0519::p1, alce::row_0519::p10, alce::row_0519::p37, alce::row_0519::p85

### `row_0523`
> # Compressing/Decompressing Files

Compression and decompression is a process where software modifies the on and off signals (bytes) that make up computer files so they take up less space on storage disks or tapes. [alce::row_0523::p18]
> Most software purchased or downloaded from the Internet is compressed. [alce::row_0523::p18]
> Compressing files is beneficial because it can dramatically reduce the file size, which is particularly useful when sending files as it reduces mobile data usage. [alce::row_0523::p64]
> There are commercial and shareware programs available that will compress and decompress files for you. [alce::row_0523::p18]
> Some popular forms of data compression include zip, tar, and stuffit, among others. [alce::row_0523::p18]
> To compress files using programs like Winrar, you simply choose the files you want to compress and select your desired compression method from options such as normal, good, or best. [alce::row_0523::p28]
> Winrar offers three different formats for compression: RAR, RAR5, and ZIP. [alce::row_0523::p28]
> However, on Android devices, there is no integrated option for compressing files, so you must use a third-party app to compress or decompress files. [alce::row_0523::p64]

### `row_0546`
> # The Cosmological Principle

The cosmological principle is the notion that the spatial distribution of matter in the universe is homogeneous and isotropic when viewed on a large enough scale. [alce::row_0546::p18]
> The cosmological principle is a strengthening of the Copernican principle, and states that on the large scale, the universe can be modeled by a solution to Einstein's equations which admits a foliation by Cauchy hypersurfaces each of which is homogeneous and isotropic. [alce::row_0546::p36]
> The Perfect Cosmological Principle is an extension of the cosmological principle, and states that the universe is homogeneous and isotropic in space and time. [alce::row_0546::p18]
> The cosmological principle was invoked by Alexander Friedmann and Abbé Georges Lemaître when they derived their solution in the 1920s. [alce::row_0546::p12]
> Standard Big Bang Friedmann-Lemaître cosmology, derived from Einstein's field equations, requires the cosmological principle to be true for it to be a valid solution of those field equations. [alce::row_0546::p12]
> The cosmological principle is a theoretical idea that is used to give structure and meaning to observations, to organize observations into a theoretical whole, rather than a principle that can be proved or disproved by evidence. [alce::row_0546::p33]

Cited: alce::row_0546::p12, alce::row_0546::p18, alce::row_0546::p33, alce::row_0546::p36

### `row_0554`
> Your voice sounds different under different conditions because of how sound travels to your ears through different pathways.

When you speak, sound reaches your inner ear through two paths: air-conducted sound traveling through the air, and bone-conducted sound traveling directly through the tissues of your head. [alce::row_0554::p61]
> The voice you hear when you speak is the combination of sound carried along both paths. [alce::row_0554::p61]
> Bone-conducted sound is particularly important because solid objects transmit the intensity of lower frequencies better than air does, so the bones in your head amplify the lower frequencies of your voice. [alce::row_0554::p18]
> This is why your voice sounds higher and more nasal when you hear a recording of yourself, since recordings eliminate the bone-conducted pathway and you hear only the air-conducted component. [alce::row_0554::p18]
> Additionally, your voice sounds different to you than to others because of head resonance—the effect of your mouth, nasal passages, and sinuses on how you perceive your own voice. [alce::row_0554::p49]

Cited: alce::row_0554::p18, alce::row_0554::p49, alce::row_0554::p61

> Regarding current progress, quantum computing is still in the experimental stage. [alce::row_0564::p25]

> Small quantum computing systems have demonstrated that they can calculate numeric results and simulate physical systems far beyond what humans can do by hand, but these are not widely used or tested other than in experimental technical labs in universities. [alce::row_0564::p3]

### `row_0566`
> # Moneyball Explained

You're right—Moneyball is about recruiting players based on statistics. [alce::row_0566::p64]
> The concept involves giving each statistic a score and comparing players to determine who offers the best value to recruit. [alce::row_0566::p46]
> ## How It Works

Moneyball uses an analytical, evidence-based approach to build a roster, and it's particularly useful for cash-strapped teams because it typically identifies undervalued, overlooked players. [alce::row_0566::p46]
> Rather than relying on traditional measures like batting average and runs batted in, the Moneyball approach emphasizes statistics such as on-base percentage and slugging percentage as better indicators of effectiveness. [alce::row_0566::p76]
> ## Sports Example

The Oakland Athletics first pioneered this approach, using on-base percentage and slugging percentage instead of traditional statistics to pick up players that other teams had overlooked, usually with great success. [alce::row_0566::p76]
> The 2002 Oakland A's, managed by GM Billy Beane, used this concept to take their less-than-talented roster with minimal payroll to the playoffs, achieving a 20-game win streak. [alce::row_0566::p47]
> The Athletics made the postseason each year from 2000 to 2003, going a stunning 103-59 in one season. [alce::row_0566::p46]
> ## The Movie

The film *Moneyball* depicts this true story, showing how Beane and Yale graduate Peter Brand compiled a roster based on unusual hidden statistics that other teams didn't pay attention to. [alce::row_0566::p47]

Cited: alce::row_0566::p46, alce::row_0566::p47, alce::row_0566::p64, alce::row_0566::p76

### `row_0572`
> # The IS-LM Model in Economics

The IS-LM model stands for "investment-savings" (IS) and "liquidity preference-money supply" (LM) and is a Keynesian macroeconomic model that shows how the market for economic goods interacts with the loanable funds market or money market. [alce::row_0572::p1]
> The model is represented as a graph in which the IS and LM curves intersect to show the short-run equilibrium between interest rates and output. [alce::row_0572::p1]
> The IS-LM model describes how aggregate markets for real goods and financial markets interact to balance the rate of interest and total output in the macroeconomy. [alce::row_0572::p1]
> The model also shows the relationship between interest rates and assets market, with the intersection of the IS and LM curves modeling "general equilibrium" where simultaneous equilibria occur in both the goods and asset markets. [alce::row_0572::p5]
> The IS-LM model can be interpreted in two ways: first, it explains changes in national income when the price level is fixed in the short-run; second, it shows why an aggregate demand curve can shift. [alce::row_0572::p5]
> An extension of the IS-LM model is the Mundell-Fleming model (also known as the IS-LM BP model), which deals with the interaction of goods and asset markets in an open economy, whereas the original IS-LM model dealt with these markets in a closed economy. [alce::row_0572::p3]

Cited: alce::row_0572::p1, alce::row_0572::p3, alce::row_0572::p5

### `row_0581`
> # Confidence Intervals: What They Are and What They Do

Confidence intervals are a statistical method used to estimate unknown parameters based on sample data. [alce::row_0581::p25]
> They provide a range of plausible values for an unknown parameter calculated from a given set of sample data.
> A practical example illustrates their purpose: if you want to estimate the average weight of everyone in New York City but cannot survey the entire population, you would instead collect data from a sample (such as 20 people), calculate the sample average, and use a confidence interval to estimate the true population parameter. [alce::row_0581::p79]
> Formally, confidence intervals were developed by Neyman (1937) as a method for researchers to estimate a parameter by performing an experiment, collecting relevant data, and computing two numbers that form the interval. [alce::row_0581::p25]
> The confidence interval increases or decreases based on the strength of evidence supporting the parameter estimate. [alce::row_0581::p74]
> It is important to note that confidence intervals are often misunderstood; they do not necessarily provide measures of the probability that an interval contains the true value, nor do they necessarily yield assessments of the likelihood or plausibility of parameter values. [alce::row_0581::p25]

Cited: alce::row_0581::p25, alce::row_0581::p74, alce::row_0581::p79

### `row_0592`
> Jupiter's day is only 10 hours long because Jupiter rotates faster than any other planet in our solar system. [alce::row_0592::p1]
> The reason for this rapid rotation is Jupiter's high rotational velocity, which is 28,148.15 mph at the equator. [alce::row_0592::p27]
> Jupiter's year is equivalent to 12 Earth years because Jupiter takes 12 Earth years to complete one revolution around the sun. [alce::row_0592::p1]
> Since Jupiter is much farther from the sun than Earth, it must travel a much longer orbital path, which takes considerably more time to complete. [alce::row_0592::p17]
> In summary, Jupiter's short day and long year are determined by two different motions: its rapid rotation on its axis (which determines day length) and its slow orbit around the sun (which determines year length).

### `row_0596`
> The Federal Reserve's status as "private" is complex and disputed.

The Federal Reserve System is described as "an odd mixture of public and private, neither strictly a government agency nor strictly a private entity either, nor is it a for-profit entity." [alce::row_0596::p17]
> It has a Board of Governors appointed by the President and confirmed by the Senate, but consists of 12 regional Federal Reserve Banks with private member banks. [alce::row_0596::p17]
> Courts have ruled on this question with varying conclusions. The U.S. Supreme Court ruled in 1928 that "Instrumentalities like the national banks or the federal reserve banks, in which there are private interests, are not departments of the government. They are private corporations in which the government has an interest." [alce::row_0596::p2]
> In 1982, a court ruled that Federal Banks are "independent, privately owned and locally controlled corporations" with insufficient federal government control over their day-to-day operations to be classified as federal entities. [alce::row_0596::p40]
> However, the Federal Reserve's own website "insists that it is not a private corporation, is not operated for profit, and is not funded by Congress." [alce::row_0596::p24]
> In summary: the sources support that the Federal Reserve has significant private elements and has been legally classified as containing private interests, though it also has public governance components, making it neither purely private nor purely public.

Cited: alce::row_0596::p17, alce::row_0596::p2, alce::row_0596::p24, alce::row_0596::p40

### `row_0597`
> ## How AES Works

AES works by first dividing data into blocks. [alce::row_0597::p82]
> The plaintext (the information to be encrypted) is separated into blocks as the first step of the encryption process. [alce::row_0597::p82]
> ## Comparison to Serpent and Twofish

Serpent, Twofish, and AES (Rijndael) were all finalists in the AES competition. [alce::row_0597::p46]
> Rijndael was ultimately selected as the AES standard after heavy competition and testing, with NIST announcing this selection on October 2, 2000. [alce::row_0597::p46]
> Like Twofish, AES operates on 128-bit blocks and supports key lengths of 128, 192, or 256 bits. [alce::row_0597::p32]
> Serpent can work with different combinations of key lengths and uses 128-bit blocks with 32 rounds in its typical implementation. [alce::row_0597::p13]
> AES has become the most widely used encryption standard for protecting network traffic, personal data, and corporate IT infrastructures worldwide. [alce::row_0597::p46]
> Serpent and Twofish remain available as alternative symmetric cryptosystems, but they were not selected over Rijndael/AES.

Cited: alce::row_0597::p13, alce::row_0597::p32, alce::row_0597::p46, alce::row_0597::p82

### `row_0599`
> Short sellers profit from falling stock prices through the following mechanism:

A trader borrows shares of a stock from a broker and sells them at the current market price. [alce::row_0599::p8]
> If the stock price then declines as the trader anticipated, the trader buys back the same number of shares at the lower price. [alce::row_0599::p8]
> The profit is the difference between the higher sale price and the lower repurchase price. [alce::row_0599::p50]
> In essence, short sellers aim to sell their shares when the price is high and buy them back when the price declines. [alce::row_0599::p83]
> This allows traders to profit from an equity whose price falls, in contrast to traditional long positions where a stock is purchased, rises in value, and is then sold. [alce::row_0599::p3]

Cited: alce::row_0599::p3, alce::row_0599::p50, alce::row_0599::p8, alce::row_0599::p83

> This indexing approach is similar to the indexing process on your local computer, but on a much larger scale. [alce::row_0621::p3]

### `row_0625`
> # Differences between Router vs. Switch vs. Hub

**Basic Definition:**
Routers, hubs, and switches are all computer networking devices used to connect two or more computers across a single network or across multiple networks. [alce::row_0625::p0]
> **Hub:**
Hubs are dumb devices that simply repeat everything they hear. [alce::row_0625::p4]
> When one computer sends a signal to the hub, the hub sends the identical signal back out to all of the other ports on the hub. [alce::row_0625::p4]
> **Switch:**
Switches have more intelligence than hubs. [alce::row_0625::p30]
> Unlike hubs, a switch can identify the destination of data and forward it appropriately, rather than broadcasting to all ports.

**Router:**
Routers are the most intelligent of the three devices. [alce::row_0625::p30]
> Routers are used to connect multiple networks together and can direct data between different networks.

**Key Distinction:**
The fundamental difference between these three devices can be characterized as "Intelligence." [alce::row_0625::p30]
> Hubs operate at the most basic level, switches add intelligence for local network switching, and routers provide the highest level of intelligence for directing traffic across multiple networks.

Cited: alce::row_0625::p0, alce::row_0625::p30, alce::row_0625::p4
